</div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>The establishment of steady-state continuous crop production during long-term deep space missions is critical for providing consistent nutritional and psychological benefits for the crew, potentially improving their health and performance. Three technology demonstrations were completed achieving simultaneous multi-species plant growth and the concurrent use of two Veggie units on the International Space Station (ISS). Microbiological characterization using molecular and culture-based methods was performed on leaves and roots from two harvests of three leafy greens, red romaine lettuce (<em>Lactuca sativa</em> cv. â€˜Outredgeousâ€™); mizuna mustard, (<em>Brassica rapa</em> var <em>japonica</em>); and green leaf lettuce, (<em>Lactuca sativa</em> cv. Waldmannâ€™s) and associated rooting pillow components and Veggie chamber surfaces. Culture based enumeration and pathogen screening indicated the leafy greens were safe for consumption. Surface samples of the Veggie facility and plant pillows revealed low counts of bacteria and fungi and are commonly isolated on ISS. Community analysis was completed with 16S rRNA amplicon sequencing. Comparisons between pillow components, and plant tissue types from VEG-03D, E, and F revealed higher diversity in roots and rooting substrate than the leaves and wick. This work provides valuable information for food production-related research on the ISS and the impact of the plant microbiome on this unique closed environment.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> veggie, ISS, microbiome, lettuce, mizuna, phyllosphere, rhizosphere, microgravity</p></section></section><section id="sec1-life-11-01060"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">1. Introduction</h2>
<p>Future space missions and habitats will likely include crop production systems designed to provide a fresh, nutritious component to the crew diet. Understanding the complexities of plant growth systems designed with the unique challenges of space exploration missions and the associated plant microbiome is essential for sustaining the health of crop systems as well as the human consumer [<a href="#B1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-life-11-01060">1</a>,<a href="#B2-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-life-11-01060">2</a>]. Molecular biology methods to characterize whole microbial communities through omics approaches have led to the understanding of organism and habitat-specific microbiomes. Plants and their associated microbiota exist in a complex association with important functions critical to plant growth and health. Microbes are recruited and assembled on or in plant tissues and organs from reservoirs such as the soil, rhizosphere, and phyllosphere [<a href="#B3-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-life-11-01060">3</a>,<a href="#B4-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-life-11-01060">4</a>,<a href="#B5-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5-life-11-01060">5</a>,<a href="#B6-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-11-01060">6</a>]. Soil microbial communities are highly diverse and are horizontally transferred to the root where, at the rhizosphere, a dynamic and complex ecosystem develops [<a href="#B7-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B7-life-11-01060">7</a>,<a href="#B8-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-life-11-01060">8</a>,<a href="#B9-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-life-11-01060">9</a>,<a href="#B10-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-life-11-01060">10</a>,<a href="#B11-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11-life-11-01060">11</a>,<a href="#B12-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B12-life-11-01060">12</a>]. Initial soil microbiome composition can influence plant pathogen interaction and disease resistance [<a href="#B13-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13-life-11-01060">13</a>]. A plantâ€™s phyllosphere hosts a diverse community of microbes that can also influence plant health and physiology [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>,<a href="#B15-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-life-11-01060">15</a>,<a href="#B16-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-life-11-01060">16</a>,<a href="#B17-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-life-11-01060">17</a>]. Plant colonizing microorganisms are affected not only by abiotic environmental conditions such as CO<sub>2</sub> concentration, temperature, spectral radiation, and humidity, but specific host leaf surface characteristics [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>,<a href="#B18-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-life-11-01060">18</a>,<a href="#B19-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19-life-11-01060">19</a>]. Studies have shown inter-plant species variability in the phyllosphere microbiome [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>,<a href="#B16-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-life-11-01060">16</a>,<a href="#B20-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20-life-11-01060">20</a>] and microbial colonization of the phylloplane is dependent on transmission of organisms from the environment such as aerosols, soils or other plant growth medium, and animal contact [<a href="#B21-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-life-11-01060">21</a>,<a href="#B22-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-life-11-01060">22</a>,<a href="#B23-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-life-11-01060">23</a>,<a href="#B24-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-life-11-01060">24</a>]. Conversely, it has been demonstrated that plants can influence the microbiome of the indoor built environment significantly contributing to the bacterial and fungal abundance and diversity on the surrounding surfaces [<a href="#B23-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-life-11-01060">23</a>]. </p>
<p>The International Space Station (ISS) is an inimitable, closed environment that has been continuously inhabited by astronauts starting with Expedition 1 in November of 2000. Unlike terrestrial, built environments, the exchange of microbes from outside the ISS is infrequent and primarily limited to the exchange of international crews after months of residency on the space station, along with re-supply missions. Since 2014, plants, primarily leafy greens, have been grown in the NASA Veggie hardware which are small growth chambers on the ISS (ORBITEC now Sierra Space., Madison, WI, United States) [<a href="#B25-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-life-11-01060">25</a>,<a href="#B26-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-life-11-01060">26</a>]. Fifteen technology demonstrations directed by NASA to grow a variety of crops have been completed to date and currently there are two Veggie units (Serial number (SN) 001 and Serial number 002) in operation on ISS. The Veggie chambers (<a href="#life-11-01060-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>) utilize a fan to circulate the air from outside of the chamber throughout the plant growth area facilitating an exchange of airborne microbes between the ISS atmosphere and the chamber interior. Air drawn from the ISS cabin flows from the bottom of the plant canopy to the top of the growth chamber volume. Air filtration is not present apart from screens to filter out large particles within the Veggie facility before entering the cabin [<a href="#B27-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27-life-11-01060">27</a>]. Environmental conditions in which ISS grown crops have been cultivated are unlike terrestrial counterparts, in addition to an absence of gravity and natural convection. Carbon dioxide levels can reach approximately 7 to 10 times higher than ambient earth atmosphere in the closed atmosphere of the ISS, temperatures are typically between 18 Â°C and 23 Â°C and humidity is low (40â€“50%), however temperature and humidity can be higher inside the Veggie chamber. A collapsible, transparent bellows contains the plant growing area and any plant debris. During the on-orbit operation, the plant crops are tended, harvested, and consumed by the crew and are intended to provide a nutritional supplement to their diet. At the completion of a harvest, all plant growth containers, known as plant pillows, are discarded, or stored frozen for return to Earth and the bellows and other surfaces are cleaned with disinfectant wipes in preparation for the next crop. </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f001"><h3 class="obj_head">Figure 1.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g001.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/0e4afbba92a3/life-11-01060-g001.jpg" loading="lazy" height="266" width="749" alt="Figure 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>A</strong>) VEG-03D (SN 002) with ambient light and bellows down. (<strong>B</strong>) VEG-03E (SN 001) with bellows lowered and plant base plate raised to a more vertical position and VEG-03F (SN 002) partially visible on the right of VEG-03E with bellows raised.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Microbial analyses using culture-based and culture-independent methods of Veggie-grown crops and growth chamber components have been completed to provide baseline data to ensure the microbiological safety for consumption by the astronauts. Subsequently, an in-depth characterization of bacterial and fungal communities associated with three crop grow-outs of red romaine lettuce that may impact astronaut and plant health has been reported [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>].</p>
<p>Plants grown in Veggie are susceptible to abiotic factors inherent in microgravity plant cultivation such as water stress and humidity control. Without careful control and monitoring, the environmental conditions within Veggie can promote undesirable levels of microbial growth. <em>Zinnia hybrida</em>, a flowering plant grown in Veggie in 2015â€“2016 became infected with the plant opportunistic pathogen <em>Fusarium oxysporum</em> after failure of the ventilation system led to an increase in humidity inside the chamber [<a href="#B29-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-life-11-01060">29</a>]. The source of the fungus was not determined definitively, however <em>F. oxysporum</em> has been isolated on surfaces on the ISS [<a href="#B29-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-life-11-01060">29</a>,<a href="#B30-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-life-11-01060">30</a>,<a href="#B31-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-life-11-01060">31</a>,<a href="#B32-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-life-11-01060">32</a>,<a href="#B33-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33-life-11-01060">33</a>]. Furthermore, <em>F. oxysporum</em> was among the fungal isolates identified from leaves and roots from two out of three Veggie demonstrations growing red romaine lettuce [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>]. The introduction of microbes from the air, water, or human interaction onto the plant surfaces of an edible crop potentially establishes a risk to the consumer. Crew safety and health is of paramount consideration when supplying a fresh food source through Veggie crop production and the characterization of the microbial communities in Veggie furthers the assurance that these crops are appropriate and safe for human consumption.</p>
<p>The VEG-03D, E, and F series of technology demonstrations discussed in this work are the first mixed crop use of the Veggie facility with the growth of three leafy greens, red romaine lettuce (<em>Lactuca sativa</em> cv. â€˜Outredgeousâ€™); mizuna mustard, (<em>Brassica rapa</em> var <em>japonica)</em>; and green leaf lettuce, (<em>Lactuca sativa</em> cv. â€˜Waldmannâ€™s Greenâ€™). </p>
<p>In addition to the demonstration of a mixed cultivar approach and continuous harvest using two Veggie systems, the objective of this study was to characterize and compare the bioburden and composition of the microbial communities within the Veggie plant growth units at different locations including above ground plant tissues, roots of 3 plant cultivars, Veggie facility surfaces, and plant pillow materials. We identified through culture- based and culture-independent methods dominant and shared genera of bacteria and fungi in plants, growth substrate, and Veggie facility surfaces. This study demonstrated simultaneous multi-species leafy green growth and the influence of the plant leaf and root associated microbial communities across plant species and Veggie growth chamber components.</p></section><section id="sec2-life-11-01060"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">2. Materials and Methods</h2>
<p>The VEG-03D, VEG-03E, and VEG-03F technical demonstrations aboard ISS present a mixed, multi-crop study with the inclusion of a â€œcut-and-come-againâ€ component where plants were partially harvested and then re-grown to yield more produce for a given the amount of resources and to determine effects on the harvest. A total of four harvests were conducted for each grow-out, with the second and half of the fourth/final harvest preserved for later microbial analysis, and the remaining harvests consumed by astronauts. The leafy greens included were red romaine lettuce (<em>Lactuca sativa</em> cv. â€˜Outredgeousâ€™); mizuna mustard, (<em>Brassica rapa</em> var japonica); and green leaf lettuce (<em>Lactuca sativa</em> cv. â€˜Waldmannâ€™s Greenâ€™). Three separate grow-outs were conducted in the two Veggie production systems on ISS with initiation in September 2017 (VEG-03D) and February 2018 (VEG-03E and F). VEG-03E and F were grown three days apart in different Veggie units to test the concept of sustainable cultivation and continuous harvest for provision of fresh produce (<a href="#life-11-01060-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>).</p>
<section class="tw xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-t001"><h3 class="obj_head">Table 1.</h3>
<div class="caption p"><p>Technical demonstration scheduling for VEG-03D, VEG-03E, and VEG-03F grown aboard ISS.</p></div>
<div class="tbl-box p" tabindex="0"><table class="content" frame="hsides" rules="groups">
<thead><tr>
<th align="center" valign="middle" style="border-top:solid thin;border-bottom:solid thin" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Tech Demo</th>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/life-11-01060-t001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section><section id="sec2dot1-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.1. Seed Preparation </h3>
<p>Seeds for each crop were surface sanitized using a chlorine gas fuming method as described by Massa et al. [<a href="#B34-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-life-11-01060">34</a>]. Briefly, seeds were batch sanitized by adding 0.5 mL HCL to 30 mL bleach in a 250 mL container by placing an open Petri dish containing the seeds into the container, without submersion, and sealing. Seeds were sanitized for one hour, removed from the container and allowed to off-gas overnight. Seed germination tests were performed to verify seed viability. Sanitization was confirmed for bacteria on Trypticase Soy Agar plates (TSA) (BD, Franklin Lakes, NJ, USA) and mold/fungi were confirmed on Inhibitory Mold Agar (IMA) (BD, Franklin Lakes, NJ, USA). Plates were incubated at 30 Â°C for 24â€“48 h for observation of bacterial growth and up to 120 h for fungal growth. </p></section><section id="sec2dot2-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.2. Pillow Assembly, Layout, and Processing</h3>
<p>All pillows were assembled at Kennedy Space Center, FL and prepared for flight as described in detail by Massa et al., 2017 [<a href="#B34-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-life-11-01060">34</a>]. Briefly, the Veggie pillows contained 250 mL of autoclaved, porous, arcillite substrate with controlled release fertilizer (Nutricote 18-6-8, type 100, Florikan ESA, Sarasota, FL, USA) mixed at 7.5 g/L dry substrate. Two surface sanitized seeds were attached with guar gum to germination wicks in each plant pillow. The mixed crop pillow layout for each study is shown in <a href="#life-11-01060-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>. </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f002"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 2.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g002.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/5c6edf2a032e/life-11-01060-g002.jpg" loading="lazy" height="315" width="718" alt="Figure 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f002/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Veggie technical demonstrations on ISS (VEG-03D, VEG-03E, and VEG-03F) showing the pillow and plant layout for each. The â€˜*â€™ indicates pillows returned from ISS for analysis. â€˜OLâ€™ is â€˜Outredgeousâ€™ red romaine lettuce; â€˜WGâ€™ is â€˜Waldmannâ€™s Greenâ€™ leaf lettuce; â€˜Mizunaâ€™ is mizuna mustard greens.</p></figcaption></figure><p>The Veggie production system is a small plant growth chamber. Designed and built by ORBITEC (Madison, WI, US) to grow crops in space, it is equipped with LED lighting, a passive watering system and a fan to generate airflow [<a href="#B25-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-life-11-01060">25</a>,<a href="#B26-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-life-11-01060">26</a>]. The SN 002 system was launched to the ISS in 2014 aboard Space X CRS-3, while the SN 001 unit was launched aboard Orbital ATK OA-7 in 2017. A HOBO (honest observer by onset) data logger (Onset, Bourne, MA, US) was placed in the Veggie units used for VEG-03D and VEG-03E to record humidity, dew point, and temperature data (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Figure S1</a>). HOBO data were not collected from VEG-03F.</p></section><section id="sec2dot3-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.3. On-Orbit Operations</h3>
<p>On-orbit operations for VEG-03D, E, and F were similar to previous Veggie tech demo missions [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>,<a href="#B35-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-life-11-01060">35</a>] with the exception of (1) 160 mL water injection for initiation; and (2) the root mat was installed without being filled with water to mitigate potential overwatering observed in previous missions. Because these growth tests were conducted by the crew autonomously using their own time, there were no ground control experiments conducted. </p>
<p>During VEG-03D operation, a fan anomaly occurred, causing a rise in humidity to 100% for approximately 25 days inside the Veggie facility and condensation on the bellows surface (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S1</a>). During the growth tests, wipes containing Pro-San (Microcide, Sterling Heights, MI, USA), an organic acid-based food surface sanitizer had been used one or two times to sanitize any obvious fungal growth on the surface of pillows and/or on the threads that hold the pillows together. The crew followed the Veggie tech demo protocols to harvest samples for analyses and to sanitize the surface of fresh leaves with Pro-San wipes before consumption. Pictures were taken for the Veggie team to evaluate plant health and other potential issues, and watering dates and volumes were documented.</p></section><section id="sec2dot4-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4. Surface Sample Collection </h3>
<p>Surface swab samples were collected by the crew from 8 different sites within or on the Veggie facility using self-contained sterile swabs (Becton Dickinson, Franklin Lakes, NJ, USA), three from the interior bellows, one from the upper one-third of the bellows, one in the middle, and the third from the lower portion, three plant pillow top surfaces, one from the internal fan screen, and one from the fan outlet vent ports. After sample collection, the swabs were returned to the sterile container and stored frozen at âˆ’80 Â°C in the Minus Eighty Laboratory Freezer on ISS (MELFI).</p></section><section id="sec2dot5-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.5. Sample Processing </h3>
<p>Leaves from the second and final harvests, swabs and selected plant pillows were maintained at âˆ’80 Â°C in MELFI until returned to Earth. Upon return all samples were stored at âˆ’80 Â°C until processing. Plant leaf samples and swabs were removed from the freezer and processed immediately. Pillows were thawed at 4 Â°C overnight. Substrate, wicks, and roots were removed from each pillow and divided for microbiological and molecular analyses (<a href="#life-11-01060-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f003"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 3.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g003.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/95d8977e89d0/life-11-01060-g003.jpg" loading="lazy" height="184" width="757" alt="Figure 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f003/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Veggie pillow after return from ISS; (<strong>A</strong>) top and (<strong>B</strong>) bottom, (<strong>C</strong>) root and arcillite substrate sample collection, (<strong>D</strong>) frozen leaf tissue sample.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec2dot6-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.6. Microbiological Analysis</h3>
<p>Leaf and pillow samples were placed into 50 mL centrifuge tubes with sterile phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) and 3â€“4 mm sterile glass beads, weighed then shaken for 2 cycles at 5 m/s for 30 s each on the Omni BeadRuptor (OMNI, Kennesaw, GA, USA). Swabs were placed into tubes containing sterile PBS with 0.3% Tween 80 and vortexed at high speed for 30 s before plating. All samples were diluted with PBS and plated in duplicate onto TSA for bacteria and IMA for fungi. Plates were incubated at 30 Â°C for 48 h for TSA and 72â€“120 h for IMA and enumerated. Colony phenotypes were selected from each sample and isolated on the respective agars. Bacterial colonies were identified using the Biolog Micro ID System (Biolog, Hayward, CA, USA). The MicroSEQ 16S rDNA sequencing kit was also utilized to identify bacterial isolates while fungal and yeast colonies were identified using the MicroSEQ D2 LSU rDNA kit for fungi (Thermo Fisher, Waltham, MA, USA). DNA was extracted from isolates using Prepman System for bacteria or the Qiagen Powerlyzer Soil Kit for fungi. All MicroSeq sequencing was completed on the ABI 3130 Genetic Analyzer (Thermo Fisher, Waltham, MA, USA). Bacterial and fungal DNA sequences were identified using the MicroSeq ID Software V2.0 (Bacterial Library, 2009 and Fungal Library 2011) followed by NCBI Basic Local Alignment Search Tool (BLAST).</p>
<p>Petrifilms were also used to identify and enumerate <em>Escherichia coli</em>/coliform and <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em> for microbial food safety screening (3M, St. Paul, MN, USA). Petrifilms were incubated at 35 Â°C for 24 h per manufacturerâ€™s guidelines and any colonies positive for <em>E. coli</em>/coliform and <em>S. aureus</em> were enumerated and re-isolated. Re-isolated colonies were identified using Biolog GEN III plates. To screen for <em>Salmonella</em>, 1 mL of sample extract was added to an additional volume of buffered peptone water (BPW) and incubated at 35 Â°C for 24 h. A 1 mL aliquot was then transferred into 5 mL of Rappaport-Vassiliadis (RV) broth or Tetrathionate broth (Thermo Fisher, Waltham, MA, USA) and incubated for an additional 24 h at 35 Â°C. The broth cultures were then streaked onto selective media for <em>Salmonella</em> and incubated at 35 Â°C for 24â€“48 h [<a href="#B36-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-life-11-01060">36</a>]. </p></section><section id="sec2dot7-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.7. Molecular Community Analysis</h3>
<p>Upon processing, components for all samples were placed in RNA<em>later</em> for preservation and frozen at âˆ’20 Â°C until DNA isolation was completed using the Qiagen Microbial Cell DNA Isolation Kit (Qiagen, Inc., Carlsbad, CA, USA) following manufacturerâ€™s protocol. DNA was quantified with the QUBIT 2.0 and the double stranded high sensitivity DNA assay (Invitrogen, Inc., Grand Island, NY, USA) then diluted to 0.5 ng/ÂµL for downstream polymerase chain reaction (PCR). PCR was completed for each sample in triplicate with barcoded primers from the V4 region of the 16S rRNA gene for bacteria and barcoded primers for the internal transcribed spacer (ITS) for fungi. Unique barcodes were assigned for both forward and reverse primers (300 nM) to multiplex samples [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>,<a href="#B37-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-life-11-01060">37</a>,<a href="#B38-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38-life-11-01060">38</a>]. The PCR cycling conditions in a Bio-Rad C-1000 thermocycler included enzyme activation at 95 Â°C for 5 min, followed by 30 cycles of 95 Â°C for 1 min, annealing at 58 Â°C for 1 min and 72 Â°C for 2 min for extension. A final 10 min extension at 72 Â°C concluded the PCR. PCR products were cleaned with the Min-Elute PCR Cleanup Kit to remove excess primers and dNTPs (Qiagen, Carlsbad, CA, USA), and sequenced as an equimolar library spiked with 10% PhiX DNA [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>]. Sequencing was completed as paired end on the Illumina MiSeq Next Generation Sequencer (NGS) [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>]. FastQ sequences were identified to the genus level using the RDP/GreenGenes and SILVA (bacteria) and Unite (fungal) databases. </p></section><section id="sec2dot8-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.8. Data Analysis</h3>
<p>Data from microbiological counts (log transformed) were compared using a one-way ANOVA followed by Tukeyâ€™s multiple comparisons test using GraphPad Prism version 8.0.0 for Windows (GraphPad Software, San Diego, CA, USA). Alpha diversity was determined using the Shannon Diversity Index and beta diversity of community sequencing was determined with Brayâ€“Curtis dissimilarity [<a href="#B39-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B39-life-11-01060">39</a>] using a one-way ANOVA (QIIME 2.0) [<a href="#B40-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40-life-11-01060">40</a>]. Venn diagrams were created using Venny 2.1. [<a href="#B41-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B41-life-11-01060">41</a>].</p></section></section><section id="sec3-life-11-01060"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">3. Results</h2>
<section id="sec3dot1-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1. Plant Germination and Growth</h3>
<p>VEG-03D was designed with a mixed horticulture layout of two pillows for each cultivar to examine the interaction of all three crop types (<a href="#life-11-01060-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>). In Veg-03D, we found that the more rapidly growing mizuna plants overshadowed adjacent lettuce plants, causing watering and accessibility issues for plant maintenance. Therefore, for the VEG-03E and F growth tests, the layout was adjusted for easy access, observation, and plant maintenance (<a href="#life-11-01060-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>).</p>
<p>The overall emergence rates for mizuna and red romaine lettuce were 91.6% and 83.3%, respectively. Green leaf lettuce seeds averaged 66.6 percent germination (75% in VEG-03D vs. 62.5% in VEG-03E/F) with two stunted seedlings. After thinning, one healthy green leaf lettuce seedling failed to develop further, resulting in only two out of four pillows with healthy green leaf lettuce plants in VEG-03E and F compared to two out of two pillows with healthy green leaf lettuce plants in VEG-03D. Except for the two green leaf lettuce plants, all other plants appeared healthy. Tip burn was observed later during the grow-outs in red romaine lettuce plants. In addition, older mizuna leaves also turned yellowish with a dry edge. All these signs occurred after the 30â€“31-day harvest, and increased with time, especially for mizuna, indicating a possible lack of nutrients with the age of the plant. No signs of disease were found in these plants. Water usage was relatively constant with about one liter consumed per plant per 30 days (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S1</a>). Mizuna required a slightly higher water volume to maintain hydration and plant health compared to both lettuces. These experiments were performed by the crew in a semi-autonomous manner; the water volume was adequate for maintaining plant health, but may not have been optimal, and may have varied among the crew members who tended these plants. </p></section><section id="sec3dot2-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.2. Bacterial and Fungal Counts on Leaves</h3>
<p>Aerobic plate counts (APC) of bacteria on leaves from Veg-03D, E, and F harvests of the three leafy greens were in the range of 4.3 Ã— 10<sup>2</sup> (red romaine in VEG-03E) to 3.5 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> colony-forming units (CFU) per gram (g) of leaf tissue. Average counts on mizuna leaves from both harvests grown in VEG-03D and E were not significantly different from each other, ranging from 1.4 Ã— 10<sup>3</sup> to 2.6 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> CFU/g. The average counts on green leaf lettuce across experiments D, E, and F were 7.9 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup>, 3.5 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup>, and 5.9 Ã— 10<sup>5</sup> CFU/g, respectively. Only one harvest was completed on the green leaf lettuce plant from E. Red romaine lettuce grown in VEG-03E had lower counts than those harvested from D and F (<em>p</em> â‰¤ 0.05). Average bacterial counts from red romaine lettuce leaves from D, E, and F were 6.9 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup>, 5.7 Ã— 10<sup>3</sup>, and 6.1 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> CFU/g<sup>,</sup> respectively (<a href="#life-11-01060-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f004"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 4.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g004.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/c8334c8e5921/life-11-01060-g004.jpg" loading="lazy" height="278" width="768" alt="Figure 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f004/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Bacterial counts (CFU on TSA per gram of frozen tissue) on leaves from plants grown in VEG-03D, E, and F for three crops. (<strong>A</strong>) mizuna, (<strong>B</strong>) red romaine lettuce, (<strong>C</strong>) green leaf lettuce. Open circles represent counts from the first harvest, closed circles the final harvest. Bars represent the mean and error bars are standard error of the mean. Statistical differences (<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) are indicated with an asterisk.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Yeast and mold counts from the leaves grown in VEG-03D were lowest on average on mizuna, 2.4 Ã— 10<sup>4</sup> CFU/g followed by 5.1 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> CFU/g on red romaine lettuce leaves and green leaf lettuce with the highest average counts at 9.8 Ã— 10<sup>9</sup> CFU/g, however the difference between plant types was not significant. The fungal counts were higher on the second harvest leaves from all three plant types grown in VEG-03D (<a href="#life-11-01060-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>). The fan anomaly may have contributed to this trend, which was not found in VEG-03E and F. All screening tests for potential food borne pathogens, <em>E. coli</em>, <em>S. aureus</em> and <em>Salmonella</em> sp. showed negative results.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f005"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 5.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g005.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/7bb0987e3a51/life-11-01060-g005.jpg" loading="lazy" height="275" width="778" alt="Figure 5"></a></p>

<figcaption><p>Fungal counts (CFU on IMA per gram of frozen tissue) on leaves from plants grown in VEG-03D, E, and F for three crops. (<strong>A</strong>) mizuna, (<strong>B</strong>) red romaine lettuce, (<strong>C</strong>) green leaf lettuce. Open circles represent counts from the first harvest, closed circles the final harvest. Bars represent the mean and error bars are standard error of the mean.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec3dot3-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.3. Bacterial and Fungal Counts on Pillow Components</h3>
<p>Plant pillows were investigated for bacterial and fungal counts from the wicking material, the roots, and the substrate inside the pillow. <a href="#life-11-01060-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a> illustrates a plant pillow after the plant had been harvested and before sampling including the wicking material (<a href="#life-11-01060-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>A) that initially holds the seed and wicks water from the substrate to the germinating seedling, and the substrate and roots after removal from the pillow (<a href="#life-11-01060-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>C). Four pillow samples from VEG-03D (2 red romaine, 1 mizuna and 1 green leaf lettuce), 2 from VEG-03E (mizuna) and 2 from VEG-03F (red romaine) were processed for analysis. </p>
<p>Bacterial counts on the wicking material and arcillite substrate were not influenced by plant type or Veggie facility (SN 001 or SN 002) used in the tech demo (<a href="#life-11-01060-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>A). The wick samples had APC counts ranging from 1.9 Ã— 10<sup>8</sup> to 1.8 Ã— 10<sup>9</sup> CFU/g. Average counts from the substrate samples (<em>n</em> = 3) ranged from 2.6 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> to 2.2 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> CFU/g. CFU/g values on the red romaine lettuce roots grown in VEG-03F were the lowest of the root bacterial counts, 9.3 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> and 2.6 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> compared to 3.4 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> and 7.1 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> CFU/g from the red romaine lettuce roots grown in VEG-03D. Bacterial counts from mizuna and green leaf lettuce roots were all higher than the red romaine lettuce counts. Two mizuna plant roots grown in VEG-03E yielded 8.7 Ã— 10<sup>7</sup> and 2.5 Ã— 10<sup>8</sup> CFU/g while the mizuna roots grown in VEG-03D had the highest bacterial counts of the mizuna, 2.6 Ã— 10<sup>8</sup> CFU/g. Only one sample of roots from the green leaf lettuce grown in VEG-03D was returned for analysis, yielding 1.3 Ã— 10<sup>8</sup> CFU/g bacteria. </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f006"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 6.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g006.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/be4ac59bd33d/life-11-01060-g006.jpg" loading="lazy" height="340" width="788" alt="Figure 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f006/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Bacterial (<strong>A</strong>) and fungal (<strong>B</strong>) counts (CFU per gram) on plant pillow components from VEG-03D, E, and F. Bars represent the median.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Fungal counts are illustrated in <a href="#life-11-01060-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>B. The lowest and highest counts on the wicks 4.7 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> to 2.2 Ã—10<sup>8</sup> CFU/g were from the 2 red romaine pillows from VEG-03D. Fungal counts were highest in mizuna roots and substrate from the same VEG-03E pillow with the highest bacterial counts, however, the lowest fungal counts were from the VEG-03D pillow that also had similarly high bacterial counts, indicating no correlation.</p></section><section id="sec3dot4-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.4. Bacterial and Fungal Counts on Surface Samples</h3>
<p>Swab samples were collected after the harvest was complete but before wiping down of the interior bellows with disinfectant wipes. While each sample site is a different location, distinct sample sites are the same across the three tech demos. Bacteria were recovered and cultivated from all 8 sample sites from VEG-03D, 3 out of 8 samples in VEG-03E and 5 out of 8 in VEG-03F (<a href="#life-11-01060-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). Fungi were recovered from 5, 3, and 7 samples out of 8 from VEG-03D, E, and F, respectively. Bacteria and fungi were recovered from the fan screen and at least two of the pillow surfaces across all the experiments (<a href="#life-11-01060-t003" class="usa-link">Table 3</a>). VEG-03E was run in SN 001 Veggie facility, a unit that was just installed and had not been used before on ISS, had the fewest locations with recovery of bacteria and fungi. VEG-03D and F were run in the same Veggie SN 002 facility, the older Veggie on ISS. Bacterial counts (CFU/swab) on surfaces from VEG-03D ranged from 3.5 Ã— 10<sup>1</sup> on the fan screen to 6.0 Ã— 10<sup>4</sup> on the pillow surface. Several samples from VEG-03E were below the detection limit with the exception of the fan screen and two pillow samples. VEG-03F had the highest CFUs on the pillow surfaces ranging from 1.3 Ã— 10<sup>5</sup> to 4.0 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup>/swab. Fungi from VEG-03D samples ranged from below detection to 4.5 Ã— 10<sup>3</sup> CFU per swab. The highest fungal load, 4.0 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> CFU/swab was found on samples from VEG-03F on a pillow sample (<a href="#life-11-01060-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>).</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/life-11-01060-t003/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section><section id="sec3dot5-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.5. Cultivated Bacteria and Fungi Isolate Identification</h3>
<p>Cultivation and recovery of the bacteria and fungi present in all samples was dependent on survival during sample storage as well as growth medium and incubation conditions and represents a fraction of the taxa present, however community sequencing verified the presence of each of the cultivated bacterial and fungal genera. A total of 30 bacterial genera were isolated on TSA and identified from VEG-03D, E, and F plant, pillow, and surface samples (<a href="#life-11-01060-t003" class="usa-link">Table 3</a>). Several genera were isolated from samples from all three Veggie sample sets including <em>Burkholderia</em>, <em>Chryseobacterium</em>, <em>Leifsonia</em>, <em>Microbacterium</em>, <em>Pseudomonas</em> and <em>Staphylococcus</em>. More genera were recovered and identified from VEG-03D (20) than from E or F, and eight of the 30 isolates were unique to VEG-03D samples (<a href="#life-11-01060-t003" class="usa-link">Table 3</a>). </p>
<p>The fungi, <em>Aspergillus</em> spp., <em>Fusarium oxysporum, Penicillium</em> spp. <em>and Rhodotorula</em> spp. where ubiquitous across samples from all three tech demos (<a href="#life-11-01060-t004" class="usa-link">Table 4</a>). <em>Exophiala</em> spp. was identified in only one sample from VEG-03E, the substrate, and swab samples from E and F recovered <em>Purpureocillium lilacinum.</em> All fungi, except the <em>Purpureocillium</em> isolated only from swab samples, were verified by community sequencing. Because swab samples yielded no DNA, we were not able to verify the presence of <em>Purpureocillium.</em> An additional 26 genera of fungi were identified in samples using community sequencing of the ITS region (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S5</a>).</p>
