<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/life-11-01060-t004/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section><section id="sec3dot6-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.6. Microbial Diversity</h3>
<p>Alpha and beta diversity represent the variation within and between samples. Alpha diversity of VEG-03D, E, and F, determined by the Shannon Index, ranged between 0.1 (very low) and 2.0 (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Tables S2â€“S4</a>). Arcillite substrate and root tissue had the highest diversity indices followed by the wick, then leaf tissue. Leaf tissue for all plant types had the lowest alpha diversity. Alpha diversity for the swabs taken for the three experiments fell between 0.8 and 1.4. The distribution as determined by the Pielou Evenness Index measurement indicated the genera present were not evenly distributed with values ranging from 0.3 for substrate to 0.1 for leaf tissue. This trend was consistent between all three experiments (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Tables S2â€“S4</a>).</p>
<p>Beta diversity as determined between samples with the Brayâ€“Curtis calculation in QIIME 2.0 was conceptualized as PCoA plots (<a href="#life-11-01060-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a> and <a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>Aâ€“E). Beta diversity was calculated between all leaf tissues returned from ISS (<a href="#life-11-01060-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a> and <a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S6</a>). Just over 50% of the variation between plant types could be explained in the 2 axes of the PCoA (<a href="#life-11-01060-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>). Plants from VEG-03D presented a visual clustering of leaves for all plant types with the exception one green lettuce leaf demonstrating similarity of the microbial communities. VEG-03E and F grown later do not cluster tightly indicating potential diversity differences. The corresponding Adonis statistics provides a permutation statistic of the analysis of variance which provides an R<sup>2</sup> value indicating the % variance explained by each group. The lower the R<sup>2</sup> value the more similar the samples. The R<sup>2</sup> value for all plant types (0.397) indicated that approximately 40% of the variation is derived from the influence of the tech demo (<em>p</em> = 0.001) versus only 10% (0.101) was due to the plant type. The influence of the plant type was not significant.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f007"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 7.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g007.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/64383b611680/life-11-01060-g007.jpg" loading="lazy" height="839" width="794" alt="Figure 7"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f007/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>PCoA plot represents the plant leaf tissue returned from VEG-03D, E, and F on the ISS. Plant type MM is mizuna mustard, OL is â€˜Outredgeousâ€™ red romaine lettuce and WG is â€˜Waldmannâ€™s Greenâ€™ leaf lettuce.</p></figcaption></figure><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f008"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 8.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g008.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/cc00a51b5f56/life-11-01060-g008.jpg" loading="lazy" height="524" width="790" alt="Figure 8"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f008/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>PCoA plots represent the plant and pillow components for VEG-03D, E and F tech demonstrations performed on ISS. (<strong>A</strong>) is VEG-03D mizuna mustard, (<strong>B</strong>) is red romaine lettuce, (<strong>C</strong>) is green lettuce, (<strong>D</strong>) is VEG-03E mizuna mustard and (<strong>E</strong>) is VEG-03F red romaine lettuce.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Beta diversity was also compared among the plant types to include the plant leaf and root and the Veggie pillow substrate and wick. Each crop type was considered separately within the three experiments (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>). VEG-03D contained the following: one pillow with mizuna mustard (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>A), two pillows with red romaine lettuce (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>B) and one pillow with green leaf lettuce (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>C). The PCoA of the three plant types with their associated pillow components in VEG-03D captured a high percentage of the variation within a single axis, with 67%, 76%, and 79%, respectively.</p>
<p>The PCoAs for VEG-03D indicate that the green leaf lettuce leaf microbiome was more dissimilar from the root, wick, and substrate. However, the root and substrate were similar. Approximately 99% of the variation accounted for within the pillow sample set was due to the sample type itself (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>C and <a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S6</a>). The additional two crops grown in VEG-03D displayed the same trends with root and substrate microbiomes more similar while the plant leaf microbiomes were dissimilar containing a greater amount of variation (97% and 84% for mizuna and red romaine lettuce, respectively) (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a> and <a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S6</a>). In all pillow samples, the wick, which did not cluster with root and substrate, appeared to be more variable.</p>
<p>The variation captured for the VEG-03E and F experiments was lower than seen in VEG-03D with approximately 40% of the variation explained in the first principle component for VEG-03E (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>D) and approximately 50% for VEG-03F (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>E). In these two experiments, the wick again appeared to have the greatest variance from other components. Leaf, root, and substrate were more similar. The R<sup>2</sup> values for VEG-03E indicated 63% of the variation was due to the sample type, whereas in VEG-03F, 77% of the variation was attributed to the sample type. All comparisons were significant (<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a> and <a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Table S6</a>). </p></section><section id="sec3dot7-life-11-01060"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.7. Community Characterization </h3>
<p>Community characterization of the pillow components and plant tissue (leaf, root, substrate, and wick) was analyzed at the genus level to determine unique and shared genera (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>). There were few genera common to all four components in each of the three technical demonstrations, (VEG-03D, E, F) and ranged between one and nine genera. VEG-03D green leaf lettuce plant and pillow samples had the highest number of shared genera with nine (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>A). In all three tech demos the wick component revealed a high number of unique genera between 2â€“19; the highest number of unique genera occurring in VEG-03E mizuna mustard pillow wicks (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>B). </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f009"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 9.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g009.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/9b507f5db8d3/life-11-01060-g009.jpg" loading="lazy" height="393" width="739" alt="Figure 9"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f009/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Venn diagrams revealing unique and shared genera across the 3 tech demos and crop types. (<strong>A</strong>) VEG-03D samples, (<strong>B</strong>) VEG-03E samples, (<strong>C</strong>) VEG-03F samples. Diagrams depict plant and corresponding pillow components (wick, substrate, and root tissue). Numbers are the number of genera with greater than 50 sequencing reads. The % is the percent total reads. Venn diagrams were completed with Venny v 2.1.</p></figcaption></figure><p><a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>A illustrates a high number of genera shared between wick, substrate, and root tissue in VEG-03D with 26 in mizuna, 22 and 31 in red romaine lettuce, and 27 in green leaf lettuce. The number of shared genera among VEG-03E components was higher for mizuna mustard at 31 and VEG-03F had 30 shared genera between wick, substrate, and root tissue (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>C). </p>
<p>Root tissue had few unique genera, but in all crops the root had high numbers of shared genera with substrate (6â€“28) and between root, substrate, and wick (22â€“31) which included more than 40% of the genera present (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>). Leaf tissue had a variable number of unique species with VEG-03D having the highest with 11 in red romaine lettuce, followed by 10 in mizuna in VEG-03E. </p>
<p>Overall, characterization of genera in the four plant and pillow components revealed few unique species in each of the components but indicated a higher number of shared species especially among root, substrate, and wick (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>).</p>
<p>A closer inspection of the top community constituents revealed that variations in the bacterial makeup between pillow sections (wick, substrate, and root) differed from the leaf tissue (<a href="#life-11-01060-f010" class="usa-link">Figure 10</a>, <a href="#life-11-01060-f011" class="usa-link">Figure 11</a> and <a href="#life-11-01060-f012" class="usa-link">Figure 12</a>). VEG-03D which included analysis of all three leafy green crops grown yielded several bacterial constituents seen in all plant types (<a href="#life-11-01060-f010" class="usa-link">Figure 10</a>). These included <em>Burkholderia</em>, <em>Enterobacter</em>, <em>Erwinia</em> and <em>Pseudomonas</em>. However, there were fewer numbers of genera in leaves than in the root, wick, or substrate, which is not unexpected. There were more similar constituents in the wick, substrate, and root with <em>Burkholderia</em> and <em>Novosphingobium</em> identified in each component for all crops (<a href="#life-11-01060-f010" class="usa-link">Figure 10</a>, <a href="#life-11-01060-f011" class="usa-link">Figure 11</a> and <a href="#life-11-01060-f012" class="usa-link">Figure 12</a>). The wick material appeared to present a higher variation in genera than did root or substrate.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f010"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 10.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g010.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/e67642fef200/life-11-01060-g010.jpg" loading="lazy" height="475" width="786" alt="Figure 10"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f010/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>VEG-03D technical demonstration. The top 15â€“17 genera for the Veggie pillows and corresponding plants as determined by 16S rRNA gene sequencing. (<strong>A</strong>) is Mizuna mustard; (<strong>B</strong>) is green lettuce and (<strong>C</strong>,<strong>D</strong>) are red romaine lettuce. Percent is the percent total reads of the top genera.</p></figcaption></figure><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f011"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 11.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g011.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/2225cb1ca36f/life-11-01060-g011.jpg" loading="lazy" height="259" width="781" alt="Figure 11"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f011/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>VEG-03E technical demonstration. The most abundant 15 genera for the two Veggie pillows and corresponding mizuna mustard leaf tissue. Percent is based upon the abundance of sequencing reads.</p></figcaption></figure><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-11-01060-f012"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 12.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8537831_life-11-01060-g012.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/794f/8537831/d665897c0a7f/life-11-01060-g012.jpg" loading="lazy" height="265" width="772" alt="Figure 12"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-11-01060-f012/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>VEG-03F technical demonstration. The most abundant (17) genera for the Veggie pillows and corresponding â€˜Outredgeousâ€™ red romaine lettuce leaf tissue grown on the ISS and determined by 16S rRNA gene sequencing. Percent is based upon the abundance of sequencing reads.</p></figcaption></figure><p>The VEG-03E samples and pillows returned from ISS were mizuna mustard (<a href="#life-11-01060-f011" class="usa-link">Figure 11</a>) while VEG-03F were red romaine lettuce (<a href="#life-11-01060-f012" class="usa-link">Figure 12</a>). Again, all samples contained <em>Burkholderia</em> with some samples containing up to 50% of the total abundance of sequencing reads. VEG-03E mizuna mustard pillow substrate and root tissues contained higher levels of <em>Novosphingobium</em> than seen in the VEG-03D tech demo. <em>Staphylococcus</em> was present in mizuna leaf from one VEG-03E pillow accounting for a large percent of the 15 listed and present in small fraction in the wick although was not detectable in the root or substrate. The wick material had a higher level of variability in the bacterial genera detected (<a href="#life-11-01060-f011" class="usa-link">Figure 11</a>). The VEG-03F tech demo comparing red romaine lettuce plant and pillow samples showed less variation in the bacterial genera in the wick and leaf compared to VEG-03D and E. <em>Pseudomonas</em> which was prevalent in most wick materials in VEG-03D, E and F could also be detected in leaf tissue (<a href="#life-11-01060-f010" class="usa-link">Figure 10</a>, <a href="#life-11-01060-f011" class="usa-link">Figure 11</a> and <a href="#life-11-01060-f012" class="usa-link">Figure 12</a>). <em>Ralstonia,</em> and <em>Pseudomonas</em> were also present in all samples from all three tech demos. (<a href="#life-11-01060-f010" class="usa-link">Figure 10</a> and <a href="#life-11-01060-f012" class="usa-link">Figure 12</a>). Several genera were present in VEG-03D (<a href="#life-11-01060-f010" class="usa-link">Figure 10</a>) and F (<a href="#life-11-01060-f012" class="usa-link">Figure 12</a>) but not in E. The genera <em>Enterobacter</em>, <em>Chitinophaga</em>, <em>Novosphingobium</em> and <em>Bradyrhizobium</em> are a few that were identified in all of the Veg-03D samples as well as some of the VEG-03F samples. In VEG-03D <em>Methylobacterium</em> was identified in red romaine lettuce and mizuna while <em>Janthinobacterium</em> was identified in red romaine.</p></section></section><section id="sec4-life-11-01060"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">4. Discussion</h2>
<p>The Veggie plant growth units deployed on the ISS have provided a platform to grow leafy greens for astronaut consumption since 2014. This series of tech demonstrations was the first time that mixed cropping was tested in Veggie, with three leafy greens. Plant growth was successful, however, there may have been an influence on growth based on plant type and position in the Veggie. It was observed in VEG 03-D that the mizuna canopy shaded the adjacent red romaine lettuce plant possibly limiting exposure to light and effecting maintenance. Germination was lower with the green leaf lettuce resulting in fewer and smaller plants. Mixed cropping could also have an impact on the transfer of plant-specific phyllosphere microbiota between crop types [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>,<a href="#B16-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-life-11-01060">16</a>,<a href="#B20-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20-life-11-01060">20</a>].</p>
<p>Microbiological food safety of Veggie-grown crops is assured by routine culture-based pathogen screening and bacterial and fungal enumeration on crops grown in ground analogs as well as samples returned from ISS. The bacterial and fungal count data and pathogen screening performed on the plants grown in VEG-03D, E, and F indicates from a microbial food safety perspective, the crops are low risk to the crew. With approximately 10% of a microbial community identified with culturable methods, sequencing of both bacterial and fungal constituents provides additional identification of the community microbiota [<a href="#B42-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42-life-11-01060">42</a>]. These data allow for the identification of the non-culturable microbes including the identification of beneficial microbes and microbes of concern and the confirmation of the negative culture-based screening results for <em>E. coli</em> and <em>Salmonella</em>. Many of the genera isolated and identified from the Veggie plant phyllosphere are present in terrestrially grown leafy greens. Several surveys of leafy greens revealed that <em>Pseudomonas</em> species dominate the phyllosphere. A study done on two types of leafy greens, rocket salad and lettuce, showed that <em>Pseudomonas</em>, was the most abundant genera present, followed by <em>Pantoea</em>, <em>Arthrobacter</em> and <em>Acinetobacter</em> [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>]. In this study, <em>Pseudomonas</em> sp. was identified by culture independent methods on all samples except one mizuna plant in VEG-03E. <em>Burkholderia</em> was identified in all plant samples by culture and culture independent methods. This is in contrast with other surveys of field grown leafy greens where <em>Burkholderia</em> is not a dominant member of the core microbiome [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>,<a href="#B15-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-life-11-01060">15</a>,<a href="#B16-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-life-11-01060">16</a>]. However, <em>Burkholderia</em> was found on both ISS grown and ground controls in an earlier study done on Veggie grown red romaine lettuce [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>].</p>
<p>Bacterial counts (CFU/g) from field and hydroponic grown lettuce have been reported in the range of 4 Ã— 10<sup>4</sup> to 2 Ã— 10<sup>8</sup> and fungal counts (CFU/g) between 8 Ã— 10<sup>2</sup> and 2 Ã— 10<sup>6</sup> [<a href="#B43-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-life-11-01060">43</a>,<a href="#B44-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-life-11-01060">44</a>,<a href="#B45-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45-life-11-01060">45</a>,<a href="#B46-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46-life-11-01060">46</a>,<a href="#B47-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47-life-11-01060">47</a>,<a href="#B48-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B48-life-11-01060">48</a>,<a href="#B49-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B49-life-11-01060">49</a>,<a href="#B50-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50-life-11-01060">50</a>,<a href="#B51-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-life-11-01060">51</a>]. The bacterial and fungal counts on leaves of plants grown in VEG-03D, E, and F were in the range of the reported values for farm or market produce counts of similar type. However, when compared to the analysis of red romaine lettuce from previous Veggie tech demos [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>], the bacterial and fungal counts per gram of red romaine lettuce leaf tissue from VEG-03D and F were higher. As with previous ISS grown leafy greens [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>,<a href="#B51-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-life-11-01060">51</a>] an increase in fungal counts was observed in some of the samples with repeated harvests especially in VEG-03D. VEG-03A and VEG-03D were run in the same Veggie facility (SN 002) and during plant growth, in both cases, the humidity was higher than other grow-outs. Humidity is a factor that can influence the microbial density and community in the phyllosphere and the surface of the leaf. It has been demonstrated that higher humidity and leaf wetness increases fungal abundance on plants including fungal plant pathogens [<a href="#B29-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-life-11-01060">29</a>,<a href="#B52-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52-life-11-01060">52</a>,<a href="#B53-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B53-life-11-01060">53</a>]. </p>
<p>The characterization of the spatial distribution of microbiota on the Veggie facility surfaces, plant growth pillows, and multi-species plant tissues was performed to elucidate possible sources and routes of transfer of microbes around the Veggie facility and plants. Generally, surface sample microbial count data showed low counts on the interior bellows surfaces, and below detection level counts mostly in VEG-03E. After harvest, the bellows are cleaned thoroughly by the crew with disinfectant wipes, perhaps minimizing microbial proliferation. The pillow surfaces had the highest counts of all surfaces sampled and are in close proximity with the moist wicking material, the plant and any plant debris which could facilitate inoculation and growth. Bacteria and fungi were recovered from the fan screen in all cases. The genera of bacteria and fungi cultured and identified have also been identified in studies to monitor the microbial profiles of the ISS. <em>Staphylococcus</em>, <em>Bacillus</em>, <em>Pantoea</em>, <em>Pseudomonas</em>, <em>Aspergillus</em> and <em>Penicillium</em> were all identified in an examination of ISS air filters and dust samples [<a href="#B54-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B54-life-11-01060">54</a>]. Several of the same genera from the previous study were isolated and identified from the ISS vacuum cleaner bag, and also included <em>Paenibacillus</em>, <em>Methylobacterium</em>, <em>Klebsiella</em>, and <em>Azospirillum</em> [<a href="#B55-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-life-11-01060">55</a>]. In addition, studies done on the microbial communities associated with surfaces on the ISS identify many of the same genera identified from the Veggie surface samples collected in this study [<a href="#B30-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-life-11-01060">30</a>,<a href="#B32-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-life-11-01060">32</a>]. We compared bacterial genera from Avila-Herrera et al. [<a href="#B56-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B56-life-11-01060">56</a>] comprehensive characterization of ISS surface and crew samples with the Veggie facility surfaces and leaves characterized in the current study and found 250 genera shared between the Veggie facility surfaces and ISS samples. The leaves, Veggie surfaces and ISS samples had 47 common genera suggesting microbial transfer and colonization between these locations (<a href="#app1-life-11-01060" class="usa-link">Figure S2</a>).</p>
<p>Beta diversity analysis of all plants grown in the three tech demos resulted in a tight clustering of plants from VEG-03D with the exception of one green lettuce leaf demonstrating similarity of the microbial communities within VEG-03D. VEG-03E and F grown later do not cluster tightly indicating potential diversity differences (<a href="#life-11-01060-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>). These differences could be the result of many factors, including the different configuration of the three crops in each Veggie, the environmental conditions present at the time of the grow-out, i.e., temperature, CO<sub>2</sub> and humidity, crew interaction, microbes present in the surrounding air, and irrigation water. Previous studies on field-grown leafy greens have shown that seasonal and temporal variation exists on the phyllosphere microbial communities, effected by leaf age, weather, location and soil type [<a href="#B14-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-11-01060">14</a>,<a href="#B15-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-life-11-01060">15</a>,<a href="#B57-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B57-life-11-01060">57</a>].</p>
<p>Unlike terrestrial agriculture, a sanitized, soilless substrate is used in Veggie to support root growth. It is aseptically packed into sterile â€œpillowsâ€ along with controlled release fertilizer [<a href="#B34-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-life-11-01060">34</a>]. The seeds are surface sanitized before planting, removing most epiphytes from the seed surface, and the water used to hydrate the plant pillows is relatively clean water from the ISS potable water system [<a href="#B35-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-life-11-01060">35</a>,<a href="#B58-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58-life-11-01060">58</a>]. Consequently, the horticultural practices employed in Veggie may limit the sources of microbiota contributing to the development of the plant microbiome. As a whole, the phytomicrobiome is shaped and recruited by the plant from its associated habitat [<a href="#B3-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-life-11-01060">3</a>,<a href="#B4-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-life-11-01060">4</a>,<a href="#B6-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-11-01060">6</a>,<a href="#B10-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-life-11-01060">10</a>,<a href="#B59-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B59-life-11-01060">59</a>]. Rhizosphere microbiomes assemble through the selection of soil microorganisms by the production of plant root exudates [<a href="#B60-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B60-life-11-01060">60</a>,<a href="#B61-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B61-life-11-01060">61</a>,<a href="#B62-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B62-life-11-01060">62</a>]. The Veggie soilless substrate is essentially microbe free until the introduction of water, seed germination and exposure to the ISS environment. Water and air from the ISS have been monitored by NASA for many years and two genera commonly identified from the potable water are <em>Burkholderia</em> and <em>Ralstonia</em> [<a href="#B58-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58-life-11-01060">58</a>,<a href="#B63-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B63-life-11-01060">63</a>]. The dominant bacterial genera in Veggie samples analyzed in this study, <em>Burkholderia</em>, was found across plant types and Veggie facility materials in both culture and molecular assays. The genus <em>Burkholderia</em> contains over 60 known species and was abundant in many of the Veggie samples, primarily in the pillow substrate, root tissue, and wicks, but in very low abundance or was absent in leaf tissue indicating the genus may have been introduced through the water source or possibly was present in the seed. <em>Burkholderia</em> has been identified as a potential, beneficial plant-associated microbe, however some species are implicated in human opportunistic infections [<a href="#B21-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-life-11-01060">21</a>,<a href="#B64-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-life-11-01060">64</a>,<a href="#B65-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B65-life-11-01060">65</a>] <em>Ralstonia</em>, also found in ISS water, was identified in community sequencing across all plant type roots, wicks, and substrate. Some species of <em>Ralstonia</em> have the potential to cause stress in plants or may be an opportunistic pathogen [<a href="#B66-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-life-11-01060">66</a>,<a href="#B67-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67-life-11-01060">67</a>,<a href="#B68-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B68-life-11-01060">68</a>]. <em>Pseudomonas</em> sp., present in the top 15 genera in community sequencing in all samples but one mizuna plant and pillow from VEG-03E may be a saprophytic epi-endophyte on plant tissue [<a href="#B16-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-life-11-01060">16</a>,<a href="#B64-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-life-11-01060">64</a>,<a href="#B69-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69-life-11-01060">69</a>,<a href="#B70-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B70-life-11-01060">70</a>,<a href="#B71-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B71-life-11-01060">71</a>]. It has been reported to be a potential promotor of plant growth by producing plant stimulating hormones as well as increasing disease resistance [<a href="#B71-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B71-life-11-01060">71</a>]. These demonstrations, D and F were completed in the same Veggie unit, SN 002, and therefore not surprising that similar microbes would be detected. Several genera were identified by sequencing in VEG-03D and F but not in E. The genera <em>Enterobacter</em>, <em>Chitinophaga</em>, <em>Novosphingobium</em>, and <em>Bradyrhizobium</em> are a few that were identified in all of the Veg-03D samples as well as some of the VEG-03F samples. <em>Chitinophaga</em> sp., works with certain fungal species as an endosymbiont to enhance nutrient uptake and growth [<a href="#B66-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-life-11-01060">66</a>,<a href="#B72-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B72-life-11-01060">72</a>]. The presence of <em>Chitinophaga</em> could be correlated with stress conditions and environmental disturbances [<a href="#B66-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-life-11-01060">66</a>,<a href="#B72-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B72-life-11-01060">72</a>]. In VEG-03D <em>Methylobacterium</em> was identified in red romaine and mizuna while <em>Janthinobacterium</em> was isolated in red romaine only. Recently it was reported that 4 strains of <em>Methylobacterium</em>, one a newly characterized strain, were isolated on the ISS with the Microbial Monitoring experiments [<a href="#B73-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B73-life-11-01060">73</a>]. <em>Methylobacterium</em> are involved in nitrogen fixation, phosphate solubilization, abiotic stress tolerance, plant growth promotion, and biocontrol activity against plant pathogens [<a href="#B74-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B74-life-11-01060">74</a>,<a href="#B75-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B75-life-11-01060">75</a>] and is a methylotrophic with the ability to utilize methanol produced by plants as a carbon source [<a href="#B76-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B76-life-11-01060">76</a>] <em>Janthinobacterium</em> is a soil bacterium with a wide range of metabolic activities, including antimicrobial properties and resistance to stressors such as UV radiation and cold temperatures providing a benefit to plants [<a href="#B77-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B77-life-11-01060">77</a>,<a href="#B78-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B78-life-11-01060">78</a>,<a href="#B79-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79-life-11-01060">79</a>]. During the grow outs of VEG-03E and F, the genera <em>Nevskia</em> and <em>Spirosoma</em> were present in VEG-03E but not seen in VEG-03F. This could be due to a natural variation between mizuna and red romaine lettuce as each plant may have its own native microbiota, or it may have been introduced. These bacteria were detected in root and leaf samples from previous Veggie tech demos, VEG-01A, B and VEG-03A [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>]. If the source of these bacteria is the water initially introduced into the soilless substrate and subsequently wicked to the seeds, this suggests possible mechanisms of transfer of water borne bacteria from the seed to the root or recruitment by the root from the surrounding substrate. An in-depth genomic analysis would have to be performed to elucidate possible sources of microbial transfer.</p>
<p>All the bacteria cultured and identified from root samples, <em>Burkholderia</em>, <em>Flavomonas</em>, <em>Leifsonia</em>, <em>Methylobacterium</em>, <em>Microbacterium</em> and <em>Rhizobium</em> were also identified in the wick samples. Presumably, the sources of these bacteria are the ISS water, the endophytic seed population, circulating air, and/or human interaction with the plants, therefore diversity of the root microbiome could be limited by the populations in these sources. Eleven of the 12 identified isolates found on the leaf samples were not isolated from the roots, in corroboration with the 16S rRNA analysis of community comparison which revealed no shared species between the leaf and root. In the culture-based assays, 6 of the leaf-isolated bacteria were also found on the wicks. Different parts of the plant, i.e., leaves and roots support distinct microbial communities [<a href="#B80-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B80-life-11-01060">80</a>,<a href="#B81-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81-life-11-01060">81</a>]. Patterns of genera common across the substrate, root, wicks, and leaves suggest alternate modes of bacterial and fungal transmission. For example, the substrate, roots, and wicks share the highest number of genera for all pillow sample sets tested while the leaves share a higher number of genera with the wicking material (<a href="#life-11-01060-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>). This suggests the possibility of horizontal transfer of bacteria from substrate to root or the converse. The substrate starts as a sterile matrix therefore the introduction of microbes could be primarily limited to the ISS potable water, microbes within the ISS, those harbored on the Veggie surfaces, or the endophytic seed microbiota. The seed microbiome of two of the three crops, red romaine lettuce and mizuna mustard, have been investigated at KSC in numerous studies since the inception of the Veggie technology demonstrations. The microbiome of both sanitized and unsanitized seeds indicated that in all seeds, abundance of microbial communities/genera was low and the genus <em>Thermogemmatispora</em> predominated, and then declined post-germination., In addition, <em>Trichodesmium</em> and some genera of cyanobacteria were detected along with low abundance of <em>Burkholderia</em>, <em>Acinetobacter</em>, and <em>Corynebacterium</em>. These latter three appear to persist in the plants after germination [<a href="#B82-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B82-life-11-01060">82</a>]. Very few genera are shared between the leaf and substrate and the leaf and root whereas the wick has more genera in common with the leaf than the substrate and root, indicating a vertical transfer from the above â€œgroundâ€ wick material and the shoot of the plant. Resident microbial populations on the leaf may be transported from the ISS environment, including the circulating air and human interaction. Human associated <em>Staphylococcus</em> was identified on the leaves, surfaces, and wicks from cultured isolates and for 16S community sequencing, it was the dominant bacteria among the top 15 identified in one VEG-03E mizuna leaf sample. Other potential human and plant associated bacterial genera identified were <em>Erwinia, Burkholderia</em>, and <em>Clavibacter.</em> Extensive genotyping of these isolates would be required to evaluate the strain and the ability to infect the host.</p>
<p>Four of the fungal species identified, <em>Aspergillus</em> spp., <em>Fusarium oxysporum</em>, <em>Penicillium</em> spp., <em>Rhodotorula</em> spp. were found in all tech demos. In previous studies of Veggie-grown plants, <em>Fusarium oxysporum</em> was not found on the first growth of lettuce or surfaces of Veggie from the tech demo designated VEG-01A but has since been isolated in all subsequent tech demos [<a href="#B28-life-11-01060" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-11-01060">28</a>] (unpublished data). This suggests that once bacteria or fungi become established in Veggie units and the ISS environment, unless eliminated by the cleaning and maintenance protocols, they could persist as members of the facility microbiome and consequently the plant microbiome.</p>
<p>In conclusion, characterization of the microbiome of ISS plant growth systems and crops is critical, not only for near term assurance of crop food safety, but also for the understanding and development of sustainable crop systems for space exploration missions. Sources of external contamination, i.e., irrigation water, air, and human handling can be monitored and controlled. Strategies, such as the introduction of defined beneficial microbes to enhance plant growth and prevent human and plant pathogen invaders may need to be evaluated and implemented after careful study of the impact to these unique space agricultural systems.</p></section><section id="ack1" class="ack"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Acknowledgments</h2>
